### Post by thedrezir on 2023-10-20
Hi,

I have installed ubuntu 23.10 with Btrfs file system. I can see that there is one subvolume - root with id 5

ID 256 gen 602 top level 5 path .snapshots

I would like to use snapper for managing my snapshots so my target is to create another subvolumes on /var and /home to exclude these directories from snapshots. Do you guys have any tutorial on this? I am trying to do it the google way but I ended up with an error 

root@AdamDell:/# btrfs subvolume create /home
ERROR: target path already exists: /home

I am not sure how to proceed next, is it a problem that I am creating snapshots while the system is running?

---

### Post by TheFu on 2023-10-20
/var does contain some permanent data you might want to backup. You can thank the snap team for this.  Also, if you install almost any network service daemon, it is common for the data used by those to be stored under /var/ somewhere.  For example, mariaDB (the mysql version F/LOSS people use) and any website setup static or dynamic will almost certainly have files under /var/www/.  Also virtual machines managed by libvirt will default to storing VM files in /var/lib/libvirt/ ... as another example.

```
$ sudo du -sh /var/lib/libvirt
131G    /var/lib/libvirt
```

Over 130GB of stuff there on the system I'm on now.  BTW, be carerful using BTRFS with virtual machine storage. There was an issue with it previously. I don't recall if it was corrected or everyone using BTRFS decided to live with the risk of data lose for that specific situation. Sorry.

Snapshots are meant to be created on a running system. That's sorta the entire point of them, so no, that isn't a problem.  Snapshots are useful for many purposes, but my main uses drop into 2 different needs.
a. Before making any large OS upgrade, create a snapshot to have an easy way to revert all of it quickly.
b. Before taking backups, create a snapshot to freeze the blocks that will be backed up. This ensures backup data isn't changing while the backups are taken. The whole while new and changed data is written elsewhere to be merged when the snapshot is deleted post-backup.  If you have lots of free storage, you can keep snapshots for a few days or weeks to make having points in time that can be restored into very easy.  Alas, these aren't backups. They are just frozen blocks on disk that aren't allowed to change. Eventually, perhaps in less than 1 day, the disk can completely fill up if sufficient storage for snapshot needs isn't carefully provided/considered.

Besides BTRFS, ZFS and LVM2 support snapshots that freeze blocks of storage.  Any other use of the term "snapshot" outside those 3 specific tools almost always, 99.95% of the time is a misuse and someone is claiming a backup that takes 5-120 minutes is a "snapshot".  It is a catchy term and the subtle differences between a copy that takes 2 hrs or frozen blocks which happen nearly instantaneously can be lost on uninformed admins/users.

---

### Post by MAFoElffen on 2023-10-20
TheFu is someone I respect very much on backup solutions. I'm surprised that TheFu didn't remind you of 3-2-1 in backups, where Snapshots do nt count as backups unless they are 'backed-up' to something other than the same filesystem...

I just gave 1fallen some links to '[Smith Sync]("https://github.com/ceremcem/smith-sync")' to play (last week) with that adds that other previously missing piece, to be able to use BTRFS snapshots as a backup solution, to be able to backup snapshots elsewhere. It incorporates the use of 'btrbk' to move the snapshots to other media. He sent me a link back to a great article to tweak Timeshift and how snapshots are taken by Timeshift for BTRFS...

For ZFS, I do the same, using sanoid/syncoid with ZFS snapshots to automate the use of ZFS Send/Receive of ZFS Snapshots to my backup media.

---

[IMG]https://user-images.githubusercontent.com/19932588/42392817-740dbb26-8154-11e8-9e69-84089564e707.png[/IMG]


/etc/fstab

```

/dev/disk/by-uuid/A504-4718 /boot/efi vfat defaults 0 1
# /boot/efi was on /dev/nvme0n1p2 during curtin installation
/dev/disk/by-uuid/89b7d451-aef0-4640-a920-0744fba9cb12 / btrfs defaults 0 1
# / was on /dev/nvme0n1p1 during curtin installation
# <file system> <mount point>   <type>  <options>       <dump>  <pass>
#
# that works even if disks are added and removed. See fstab(5).
# device; this may be used with UUID= as a more robust way to name devices
# Use 'blkid' to print the universally unique identifier for a
#
# /etc/fstab: static file system information.

```

### Post by #&amp;thj^% on 2023-10-21
And you say the installer left you in this mess?
That fstab is wrong in so many ways.
Will you now run the system-info script found in my signature, and post the link back here so we can see what's going on here
I will save you sometime just use this:
```
wget -N -t 5 -T 10 https://github.com/UbuntuForums/system-info/raw/main/system-info && \
chmod +x system-info && \
./system-info --details
```
That way i don't need to ask many questions.

### Post by #&amp;thj^% on 2023-10-22
> **TheFu said:**
> I suspect you mean "LVM/ext4"?  
Exactly what I meant....maybe i need to run my posts by you first for editing then.:lolflag:
To expand on this for The OP:
LVM2, the rewrite of The Linux Logical Volume Manager. LVM supports enterprise level volume management of disk and disk subsystems by grouping arbitrary disks into volume groups. The total capacity of volume groups can be allocated to logical volumes, which are accessed as regular block devices. Task: server, cloud-image, ubuntu-live, kubuntu-live, xubuntu-live, lubuntu-live, ubuntustudio-dvd-live, ubuntukylin-live, ubuntu-mate-live, ubuntu-budgie-live.

Original-Maintainer: Debian LVM Team. lvm2 is Linux Logical Volume Manager Linux Logical Volume Manager
